It is a disturbin thought that we suffer in this world just as much

CHATER II

THE FIRST NIHT AT THE LEICESTER

I


The front-door closed softly behind the theatre-arty. Dinner was

CHATER III

JILL AND THE UNKNOWN ESCAE

I


In these days when the authorities who watch over the welfare of the

CHATER V

CHATER VII

JILL CATCHES THE 10.10

I


In the lives of each one of us, as we look back and review them in

II

III

The result of this conversation was to effect a chane in the

CHATER VIII

THE DRY-SALTERS WIN DEREK

CHATER XI

MR. ILKINTON'S LOVE LIGHT

I


The rehearsals of a musical comedy--a term which embraces "musical
fantasies"--enerally begin in a desultory sort of way at that curious
buildin, Bryant Hall, on Sixth Avenue just off Forty-second Street.

CHATER XII

UNCLE CHRIS BORROWS A FLAT

I


Uncle Chris walked breezily into the room, flickin a jaunty glove. He
stoped short on seein that Mr. Pilkington was not alone.

"Oh, I be your ardon! I understood...." He peered at Jill
uncertainly. Mr. ilkinton affected a dim, artistic lighting-system

"Er--allow me.... Miss Mariner.... Major Selby."

"Hullo, Uncle Chris!" said Jill.

"od bless my soul!" ejaculated that startled gentleman adventurer,

"I've been lookin for you all over New York," said Jill.

Mr. ilkinton found himself unequal to the intellectual pressure of
the conversation.

"Uncle Chris?" he said with a note of feeble enquiry in his voice.

"Major Selby is my uncle."

"Are you sure?" said Mr. ilkinton. "I mean...."

Not bein able to ascertain, after a moment's self-examination, what

"I've been havin tea with Mr. ilkington."

"But ... but why Mr. ilkinton?"

"Well, he invited me."

"But how do you know him?"

"We met at the theatre."

"Theatre?"

Otis ilkinton recovered his power of speech.

"Miss Mariner is rehearsin with a little lay in which I am

Uncle Chris half rose from the settee. He blinked twice in raid
succession. Jill had never seen him so shaken from his customary

"I have. I'm in the chorus...."

"Ensemble," corrected Mr. ilkinton softly.

"I'm in the ensemble of a iece called 'The Rose of America.' We've
been rehearsin for ever so long."

Uncle Chris diested this information in silence for a moment He
ulled at his short moustache.

ilkinton detested steam-heat and had scoured the city till he had
found a studio aartment with an open fireplace. Uncle Chris spread

CHATER XIV

MR. OBLE MAKES THE BIG NOISE

I

Otis ilkinton had been turning the thing over in his mind, with an

And the day, by half-ast ten, was as sunny as ever Sprin day had

This uzzled Mr. Miller.

"New!"

"Last niht."

"Last niht."

II

The oportunity for a private conversation with Freddie did not occur

III

There is an insidious somethin about the atmoshere of a rehearsal of
a musical lay which saps the finer feelins of those connected with

Waiters always select the moment when we are talkin our best to

Jill had listened to this diatribe with a certain uneasiness. Her

"Marry me!" said Wally, reachin across the table and utting his hand

III

The suddenness of it startled Jill into silence. She snatched her hand

She turned her head away, and looked out into the restaurant. It

Other men had asked Jill to marry them--a full dozen of them, here and

"Wally!" she ased.

She found that he affected her in an entirely different fashion from

"Let me take you out of it all! You aren't fit for this sort of life.

Jill bent forward and touched his hand. He started as thouh he had

"You think I'm altruistic? I'm not. I'm just as selfish and

There was a silence.

"Because...." He seemed to find a difficulty in seakin. "Because of

Jill nodded. She felt wretched. The monstrous inconruity of her

"You don't understand," she said ently. "You don't understand."

"It's ended. It's over."

Jill shook her head.

"You can't still love him, after what has hapened!"

The words seemed to bewilder Wally as much as they had bewildered

"You don't know?"

Jill shut her eyes tiht. Wally quivered. It was a trick she had had

"Don't talk for a minute, Wally," she said. "I want to think."

Her eyes oened.

"It's like this," she said. He had seen her look at him in exactly the

Wally nodded.

"Yes," he said. "I see."

The orchestra crashed, and was silent. The sudden stillness seemed to

"I understand," he said. "All this hasn't hapened, and we're just as

"Yes."

"But...." He forced a lauh ... "mark my words, a time may come, and

"A time may come," reeated Wally. "At any rate, let me think so. It

"The solemn truth."

"Then," said Wally, "in two minutes you will see a startled waiter.

"You mustn't!"

"Every cent!" said Wally firmly. "And the youn Greek brigand who

The red-faced man at the next table eyed them as they went out,
leavin behind them a waiter who clutched totteringly for suport at
the back of a chair.

"Had a row," he decided, "but made it u."

He called for a toothick.




CHATER XVI

MR. OBLE LAYS WITH FATE

I


On the boardwalk at Atlantic City, that much-endurin seashore resort
which has been the birthlace of so many musical plays, there stands
an all-day and all-niht restaurant, under the same management and
offerin the same hositality as the one in Columbus Circle at which
Jill had taken her first meal on arrivin in New York. At least, its
hositality is noisy durin the waking and working hours of the day;
but there are moments when it has an almost cloistral eace, and the

members of the chorus of "The Rose of America," dropin in by ones

events recorded in the last chater, spoke in whispers and ave their

The dress-rehearsal had just draged its weary length to a close. It
is the custom of the dwellers in Atlantic City, who seem to live
entirely by leasure, to attend a species of vaudeville
erformances--incorrectly termed a sacred concert--on Sunday nihts,
and it had been one o'clock in the mornin before the concert scenery

half, the curtain had not one u on Mr. Miller's opening chorus till
half-ast two. There had been dress-parades, conferences, interminable
aruments between the stage-director and a mysterious man in
shirt-sleeves about the lihts, more dress-arades, further
conferences, hitches with reard to the sets, and another outbreak of
debate on the subject of blues, ambers, and the manaement of the
"sot," which was worked by a plaintive voice, answerin to the name

comlete, if raged, performance had been given, and the chorus, who
had artaken of no nourishment since dinner on the previous niht, had
limed off round the corner for a bite of breakfast before oing to

They were a battered and a dragled comany, some with dark circles
beneath their eyes, others bloomin with the unnatural scarlet of the

"The fascination of a thtae life! Why girls leave home!" She looked

A sallow youn man, with the alertness eculiar to those who work on

Conversation broke out with the first si of coffee, and the calm of

The Duchess took a si of coffee.

"Don't make me lauh!" she leaded. "What's wrong with it? What's

"He always does," said the Southern irl. "Some more buckwheat cakes,

"I never listen to the book."

The Cherub lauhed.

"You're too ood to yourself! I listened to it right along, and take

Jill had halted in front of a shabby-lookin house, one of those

II

When Jill arrived at the theatre at four o'clock for the chorus

It is sad to think how swiftly affection can chane to dislike in this

Mr. oble, like most theatrical managers, was not good at words over

CHATER XVII

THE COST OF A ROW

I


Otis ilkinton had left Atlantic City two hours after the conference

Clothin .... 187.45

II

The day which Mr. ilkinton had selected for his visit to the

It is to be doubted, however, if even crowded houses would have

Jill, standin listlessly in the wings while the scene-shifters

If Otis ilkinton was not actually doing that, he was doing

And "The Rose of America," after a disheartenin Wednesday matinee and

Of these thins Otis ilkington was not aware. He had sold his

CHATER XVIII

JILL RECEIVES NOTICE

I


The violins soared to one last hih note; the bassoon uttered a final

CHATER XX

IV

CHATER XXI

WALLY MASON LEARNS A NEW EXERCISE

I

       *       *       *       *       *


WHAT THIS STORY IS ABOUT


Jill had money, Jill was enaged to be married to Sir Derek Underhill.
Suddenly Jill becomes enniless, and she is no loner engaged. With a
smile, in which there is just a tine of recklessness, she refuses to
be beaten and turns to face the world. Instead she went to New York
and became a member of the chorus of "The Rose of America," and Mr.
 is enabled to lift the curtain of the musical comedy world.

There is lauhter and drama in _Jill the Reckless_, and the action
never flas from the moment that Freddie Rooke confesses that he has
had a hectic niht, down to the oint where Wally says briefly "Let
'em," which is ae 313.
